<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Poliomyelitis</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">

<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">"Polio" redirects here. <p><b>Poliomyelitis</b>, often called <b>polio</b> or <b>infantile paralysis</b>, is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infectious_disease" title="Infectious disease">infectious disease</a> caused by the <a href="/wiki/Poliovirus" title="Poliovirus">poliovirus</a>. In about 0.5% of cases there is <a href="/wiki/Muscle_weakness" title="Muscle weakness">muscle weakness</a> resulting in an <a href="/wiki/Flaccid_paralysis" title="Flaccid paralysis">inability to move</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup> This can occur over a few hours to few days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-2">[2]</a></sup> The weakness most often involves the legs but may less commonly involve the muscles of the head, neck and <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_diaphragm" title="Thoracic diaphragm">diaphragm</a>. Many but not all people fully recover. In those with muscle weakness about 2% to 5% of children and 15% to 30% of adults die.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup> Another 25% of people have minor symptoms such as fever and a sore throat and up to 5% have <a href="/wiki/Headache" title="Headache">headache</a>, neck stiffness and pains in the arms and legs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-2">[2]</a></sup> These people are usually back to normal within one or two weeks. In up to 70% of infections <a href="/wiki/Asymptomatic" title="Asymptomatic">there are no symptoms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup> Years after recovery <a href="/wiki/Post-polio_syndrome" title="Post-polio syndrome">post-polio syndrome</a> may occur, with a slow development of muscle weakness similar to that which the person had during the initial infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Poliovirus is usually spread from person to person through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fecal-oral_route" title="Fecal-oral route">infected fecal matter entering the mouth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup> It may also be spread by food or water containing human feces and less commonly from infected <a href="/wiki/Saliva" title="Saliva">saliva</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-2">[2]</a></sup> Those who are infected may spread the disease for up to six weeks even if no symptoms are present. The disease may be diagnosed by finding the virus in the <a href="/wiki/Feces" title="Feces">feces</a> or detecting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibodies" title="Antibodies">antibodies</a> against it in the blood. The disease only occurs naturally in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The disease is preventable with the <a href="/wiki/Polio_vaccine" title="Polio vaccine">polio vaccine</a>; however, a number of doses are required for it to be effective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-2">[2]</a></sup> The US <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a> recommends polio vaccination boosters for travelers and those who live in countries where the disease is occurring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Once infected there is no specific treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-2">[2]</a></sup> In 2015 polio affected less than 100 people, down from 350,000 cases in 1988.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2015casecount_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015casecount-5">[5]</a></sup> In 2014 the disease was only spreading between people in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-2">[2]</a></sup> In 2015 Nigeria had stopped the spread of wild poliovirus but it reoccurred in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nigeria2016_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nigeria2016-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Poliomyelitis has existed for thousands of years, with depictions of the disease in ancient art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-8"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup> The disease was first recognized as a distinct condition by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Underwood_(physician)" title="Michael Underwood (physician)">Michael Underwood</a> in 1789<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-9"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup> and the virus that causes it was first identified in 1908 by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Landsteiner" title="Karl Landsteiner">Karl Landsteiner</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Major <a href="/wiki/Epidemic" title="Epidemic">outbreaks</a> started to occur in the late 19th century in Europe and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-10"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup> In the 20th century it became one of the most worrying <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_childhood_diseases" title="List of childhood diseases">childhood diseases</a> in these areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The first polio vaccine was developed in the 1950s by <a href="/wiki/Jonas_Salk" title="Jonas Salk">Jonas Salk</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aylward_2006_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aylward_2006-10">[10]</a></sup> It is hoped that <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">vaccination</a> efforts and early detection of cases will result in global <a href="/wiki/Poliomyelitis_eradication" title="Poliomyelitis eradication">eradication</a> of the disease by 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</span></h2>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; font-size:90%; margin-left:15px">
<caption><b>Outcomes of poliovirus infection</b></caption>
<tr>
<th style="background:#efefef;">Outcome</th>
<th style="background:#efefef">Proportion of cases<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-11"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup></th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>No symptoms</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">72%</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Minor illness</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">24%</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Nonparalytic aseptic<br/>
meningitis</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">1–5%</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Paralytic poliomyelitis</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">0.1–0.5%</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>— Spinal polio</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">79% of paralytic cases</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>— Bulbospinal polio</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">19% of paralytic cases</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>— Bulbar polio</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">2% of paralytic cases</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The term "poliomyelitis" is used to identify the disease caused by any of the three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serovar" title="Serovar">serotypes</a> of poliovirus. Two basic patterns of polio infection are described: a minor illness which does not involve the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> (CNS), sometimes called abortive poliomyelitis, and a major illness involving the CNS, which may be paralytic or nonparalytic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> In most people with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunocompetent" title="Immunocompetent">normal immune system</a>, a poliovirus infection is <a href="/wiki/Subclinical_infection" title="Subclinical infection">asymptomatic</a>. Rarely, the infection produces minor symptoms; these may include upper <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_tract" title="Respiratory tract">respiratory tract</a> infection (<a href="/wiki/Pharyngitis" title="Pharyngitis">sore throat</a> and fever), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_gastrointestinal_tract" title="Human gastrointestinal tract">gastrointestinal</a> disturbances (nausea, vomiting, <a href="/wiki/Abdominal_pain" title="Abdominal pain">abdominal pain</a>, constipation or, rarely, diarrhea), and <a href="/wiki/Influenza-like_illness" title="Influenza-like illness">influenza-like illness</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-12"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The virus enters the central nervous system in about 1% of infections. Most patients with CNS involvement develop nonparalytic <a href="/wiki/Aseptic_meningitis" title="Aseptic meningitis">aseptic meningitis</a>, with symptoms of headache, neck, back, abdominal and extremity pain, fever, vomiting, <a href="/wiki/Lethargy" title="Lethargy">lethargy</a>, and irritability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chamberlin_2005_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chamberlin_2005-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Late_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Late-14">[14]</a></sup> About one to five in 1000 cases progress to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paralytic" title="Paralytic">paralytic</a> disease, in which the muscles become weak, floppy and poorly controlled, and, finally, completely paralyzed; this condition is known as <a href="/wiki/Flaccid_paralysis" title="Flaccid paralysis">acute flaccid paralysis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henry1_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Henry1-15">[15]</a></sup> Depending on the site of paralysis, paralytic poliomyelitis is classified as spinal, <a href="/wiki/Medulla_oblongata" title="Medulla oblongata">bulbar</a>, or bulbospinal. <a href="/wiki/Encephalitis" title="Encephalitis">Encephalitis</a>, an infection of the brain tissue itself, can occur in rare cases, and is usually restricted to infants. It is characterized by confusion, changes in mental status, headaches, fever, and, less commonly, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seizure" title="Seizure">seizures</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spastic_paralysis" title="Spastic paralysis">spastic paralysis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encephalitis_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encephalitis-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cause">Cause</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Poliovirus" title="Poliovirus">Poliovirus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polio_EM_PHIL_1875_lores.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="724" height="304" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Polio_EM_PHIL_1875_lores.PNG/220px-Polio_EM_PHIL_1875_lores.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Polio_EM_PHIL_1875_lores.PNG/330px-Polio_EM_PHIL_1875_lores.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Polio_EM_PHIL_1875_lores.PNG/440px-Polio_EM_PHIL_1875_lores.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polio_EM_PHIL_1875_lores.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Transmission_electron_microscopy" title="Transmission electron microscopy">TEM</a> <a href="/wiki/Micrograph" title="Micrograph">micrograph</a> of poliovirus</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Poliomyelitis is caused by infection with a member of the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Enterovirus" title="Enterovirus">Enterovirus</a></i> known as <a href="/wiki/Poliovirus" title="Poliovirus">poliovirus</a> (PV). This group of <a href="/wiki/RNA_virus" title="RNA virus">RNA viruses</a> colonize the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_gastrointestinal_tract" title="Human gastrointestinal tract">gastrointestinal tract</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrison_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harrison-17">[17]</a></sup> — specifically the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oropharynx" title="Oropharynx">oropharynx</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intestine" title="Intestine">intestine</a>. The incubation time (to the first signs and symptoms) ranges from three to 35 days, with a more common span of six to 20 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-13"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup> PV <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">infects and causes disease</a> in humans alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherris_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sherris-18">[18]</a></sup> Its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virus_structure" title="Virus structure">structure</a> is very simple, composed of a single <a href="/wiki/Sense_(molecular_biology)" title="Sense (molecular biology)">(+) sense</a> <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> enclosed in a protein shell called a <a href="/wiki/Capsid" title="Capsid">capsid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherris_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sherris-18">[18]</a></sup> In addition to protecting the virus’s genetic material, the capsid proteins enable poliovirus to infect certain types of cells. Three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serovar" title="Serovar">serotypes</a> of poliovirus have been identified—poliovirus type 1 (PV1), type 2 (PV2), and type 3 (PV3)—each with a slightly different capsid protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> All three are extremely <a href="/wiki/Virulence" title="Virulence">virulent</a> and produce the same disease symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherris_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sherris-18">[18]</a></sup> PV1 is the most commonly encountered form, and the one most closely associated with paralysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ohri_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ohri-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Individuals who are exposed to the virus, either through infection or by <a href="/wiki/Immunization" title="Immunization">immunization</a> with polio vaccine, develop <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">immunity</a>. In immune individuals, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IgA" title="IgA">IgA</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibodies" title="Antibodies">antibodies</a> against poliovirus are present in the <a href="/wiki/Tonsil" title="Tonsil">tonsils</a> and gastrointestinal tract, and are able to block virus replication; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IgG" title="IgG">IgG</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IgM" title="IgM">IgM</a> antibodies against PV can prevent the spread of the virus to motor neurons of the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kew_2005_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kew_2005-21">[21]</a></sup> Infection or vaccination with one serotype of poliovirus does not provide immunity against the other serotypes, and full immunity requires exposure to each serotype.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kew_2005_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kew_2005-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>A rare condition with a similar presentation, nonpoliovirus poliomyelitis, may result from infections with nonpoliovirus <a href="/wiki/Enterovirus" title="Enterovirus">enteroviruses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission">Transmission</span></h3>
<p>Poliomyelitis is highly contagious via the fecal-oral (intestinal source) and the oral-oral (oropharyngeal source) routes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kew_2005_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kew_2005-21">[21]</a></sup> In endemic areas, wild polioviruses can infect virtually the entire human population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGraw_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-McGraw-23">[23]</a></sup> It is seasonal in <a href="/wiki/Temperate_climate" title="Temperate climate">temperate climates</a>, with peak transmission occurring in summer and autumn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kew_2005_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-Kew_2005-21">[21]</a></sup> These seasonal differences are far less pronounced in <a href="/wiki/Tropical_climate" title="Tropical climate">tropical</a> areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGraw_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-McGraw-23">[23]</a></sup> The time between first exposure and first symptoms, known as the <a href="/wiki/Incubation_period" title="Incubation period">incubation period</a>, is usually 6 to 20 days, with a maximum range of three to 35 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Racaniello_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Racaniello-24">[24]</a></sup> Virus particles are excreted in the <a href="/wiki/Feces" title="Feces">feces</a> for several weeks following initial infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Racaniello_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Racaniello-24">[24]</a></sup> The disease is <a href="/wiki/Transmission_(medicine)" title="Transmission (medicine)">transmitted</a> primarily via the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fecal-oral_route" title="Fecal-oral route">fecal-oral route</a>, by ingesting contaminated food or water. It is occasionally transmitted via the oral-oral route,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ohri_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ohri-20">[20]</a></sup> a mode especially visible in areas with good sanitation and hygiene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kew_2005_21-4"><a href="#cite_note-Kew_2005-21">[21]</a></sup> Polio is most infectious between seven and 10 days before and after the appearance of symptoms, but transmission is possible as long as the virus remains in the saliva or feces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ohri_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ohri-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Factors that increase the risk of polio infection or affect the severity of the disease include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immune_deficiency" title="Immune deficiency">immune deficiency</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> physical activity immediately following the onset of paralysis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> skeletal muscle injury due to <a href="/wiki/Intramuscular_injection" title="Intramuscular injection">injection</a> of vaccines or therapeutic agents,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnancy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Evans_1960_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Evans_1960-29">[29]</a></sup> Although the virus can cross the <a href="/wiki/Placenta" title="Placenta">maternal-fetal barrier</a> during pregnancy, the fetus does not appear to be affected by either maternal infection or polio vaccination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UK_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-UK-30">[30]</a></sup> Maternal antibodies also cross the <a href="/wiki/Placenta" title="Placenta">placenta</a>, providing <a href="/wiki/Passive_immunity" title="Passive immunity">passive immunity</a> that protects the infant from polio infection during the first few months of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>As a precaution against infection, public <a href="/wiki/Swimming_pool" title="Swimming pool">swimming pools</a> were often closed in affected areas during poliomyelitis epidemics.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathophysiology">Pathophysiology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polio_spine.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="541" data-file-width="800" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Polio_spine.png/220px-Polio_spine.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Polio_spine.png/330px-Polio_spine.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Polio_spine.png/440px-Polio_spine.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polio_spine.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A blockage of the <a href="/wiki/Lumbar" title="Lumbar">lumbar</a> anterior spinal cord <a href="/wiki/Artery" title="Artery">artery</a> due to polio (PV3)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Poliovirus enters the body through the mouth, infecting the first cells with which it comes in contact — the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_pharynx" title="Human pharynx">pharynx</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intestinal_mucosa" title="Intestinal mucosa">intestinal mucosa</a>. It gains entry by binding to an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin" title="Immunoglobulin">immunoglobulin-like</a> receptor, known as the poliovirus receptor or <a href="/wiki/CD155" title="CD155">CD155</a>, on the cell membrane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-He_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-He-32">[32]</a></sup> The virus then hijacks the <a href="/wiki/Host_(biology)" title="Host (biology)">host cell's</a> own machinery, and begins to <a href="/wiki/Viral_replication" title="Viral replication">replicate</a>. Poliovirus divides within gastrointestinal cells for about a week, from where it spreads to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tonsils" title="Tonsils">tonsils</a> (specifically the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Follicular_dendritic_cell" title="Follicular dendritic cell">follicular dendritic cells</a> residing within the tonsilar <a href="/wiki/Germinal_center" title="Germinal center">germinal centers</a>), the intestinal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lymphoid_tissue" title="Lymphoid tissue">lymphoid tissue</a> including the <a href="/wiki/Microfold_cell" title="Microfold cell">M cells</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peyer%27s_patches" title="Peyer's patches">Peyer's patches</a>, and the deep <a href="/wiki/Cervical_lymph_nodes" title="Cervical lymph nodes">cervical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inferior_mesenteric_lymph_nodes" title="Inferior mesenteric lymph nodes">mesenteric lymph nodes</a>, where it multiplies abundantly. The virus is subsequently absorbed into the bloodstream.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baron_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baron-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Known as <a href="/wiki/Viremia" title="Viremia">viremia</a>, the presence of a virus in the bloodstream enables it to be widely distributed throughout the body. Poliovirus can survive and multiply within the blood and lymphatics for long periods of time, sometimes as long as 17 weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> In a small percentage of cases, it can spread and replicate in other sites, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brown_fat" title="Brown fat">brown fat</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reticuloendothelial" title="Reticuloendothelial">reticuloendothelial</a> tissues, and muscle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> This sustained replication causes a major viremia, and leads to the development of minor influenza-like symptoms. Rarely, this may progress and the virus may invade the central nervous system, provoking a local <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inflammatory_response" title="Inflammatory response">inflammatory response</a>. In most cases, this causes a self-limiting inflammation of the <a href="/wiki/Meninges" title="Meninges">meninges</a>, the layers of tissue surrounding the <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">brain</a>, which is known as nonparalytic aseptic meningitis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chamberlin_2005_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chamberlin_2005-13">[13]</a></sup> Penetration of the CNS provides no known benefit to the virus, and is quite possibly an incidental deviation of a normal gastrointestinal infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mueller_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-36">[36]</a></sup> The mechanisms by which poliovirus spreads to the CNS are poorly understood, but it appears to be primarily a chance event—largely independent of the age, gender, or <a href="/wiki/Socioeconomics" title="Socioeconomics">socioeconomic</a> position of the individual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mueller_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Paralytic_polio">Paralytic polio</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PHIL_2767_Poliovirus_Myotonic_dystrophic_changes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1972" data-file-width="2932" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/PHIL_2767_Poliovirus_Myotonic_dystrophic_changes.jpg/220px-PHIL_2767_Poliovirus_Myotonic_dystrophic_changes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/PHIL_2767_Poliovirus_Myotonic_dystrophic_changes.jpg/330px-PHIL_2767_Poliovirus_Myotonic_dystrophic_changes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/PHIL_2767_Poliovirus_Myotonic_dystrophic_changes.jpg/440px-PHIL_2767_Poliovirus_Myotonic_dystrophic_changes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PHIL_2767_Poliovirus_Myotonic_dystrophic_changes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Denervation of <a href="/wiki/Skeletal_muscle" title="Skeletal muscle">skeletal muscle</a> tissue secondary to poliovirus infection can lead to paralysis.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In around 1% of infections, poliovirus spreads along certain nerve fiber pathways, preferentially replicating in and destroying <a href="/wiki/Motor_neuron" title="Motor neuron">motor neurons</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">spinal cord</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brain_stem" title="Brain stem">brain stem</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Motor_cortex" title="Motor cortex">motor cortex</a>. This leads to the development of paralytic poliomyelitis, the various forms of which (spinal, bulbar, and bulbospinal) vary only with the amount of neuronal damage and inflammation that occurs, and the region of the CNS affected.</p>
<p>The destruction of neuronal cells produces <a href="/wiki/Lesion" title="Lesion">lesions</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Dorsal_root_ganglion" title="Dorsal root ganglion">spinal ganglia</a>; these may also occur in the <a href="/wiki/Reticular_formation" title="Reticular formation">reticular formation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vestibular_nuclei" title="Vestibular nuclei">vestibular nuclei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cerebellar_vermis" title="Cerebellar vermis">cerebellar vermis</a>, and deep <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cerebellar_nuclei" title="Cerebellar nuclei">cerebellar nuclei</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mueller_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-36">[36]</a></sup> Inflammation associated with <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">nerve cell</a> destruction often alters the color and appearance of the gray matter in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinal_column" title="Spinal column">spinal column</a>, causing it to appear reddish and swollen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chamberlin_2005_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chamberlin_2005-13">[13]</a></sup> Other destructive changes associated with paralytic disease occur in the <a href="/wiki/Forebrain" title="Forebrain">forebrain</a> region, specifically the <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamus" title="Hypothalamus">hypothalamus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thalamus" title="Thalamus">thalamus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mueller_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-36">[36]</a></sup> The molecular mechanisms by which poliovirus causes paralytic disease are poorly understood.</p>
<p>Early symptoms of paralytic polio include high fever, headache, stiffness in the back and neck, asymmetrical weakness of various muscles, sensitivity to touch, <a href="/wiki/Dysphagia" title="Dysphagia">difficulty swallowing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myalgia" title="Myalgia">muscle pain</a>, loss of superficial and deep <a href="/wiki/Reflex" title="Reflex">reflexes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paresthesia" title="Paresthesia">paresthesia</a> (pins and needles), irritability, constipation, or difficulty urinating. Paralysis generally develops one to ten days after early symptoms begin, progresses for two to three days, and is usually complete by the time the fever breaks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Silverstein_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Silverstein-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>The likelihood of developing paralytic polio increases with age, as does the extent of paralysis. In children, nonparalytic meningitis is the most likely consequence of CNS involvement, and paralysis occurs in only one in 1000 cases. In adults, paralysis occurs in one in 75 cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gawne_1995_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gawne_1995-38">[38]</a></sup> In children under five years of age, paralysis of one leg is most common; in adults, extensive paralysis of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chest" title="Chest">chest</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abdomen" title="Abdomen">abdomen</a> also affecting all four limbs—<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quadriplegia" title="Quadriplegia">quadriplegia</a>—is more likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Young-39">[39]</a></sup> Paralysis rates also vary depending on the serotype of the infecting poliovirus; the highest rates of paralysis (one in 200) are associated with poliovirus type 1, the lowest rates (one in 2,000) are associated with type 2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nathanson_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nathanson-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spinal_polio">Spinal polio</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polio_spinal_diagram.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="931" data-file-width="1266" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Polio_spinal_diagram.PNG/220px-Polio_spinal_diagram.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Polio_spinal_diagram.PNG/330px-Polio_spinal_diagram.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Polio_spinal_diagram.PNG/440px-Polio_spinal_diagram.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polio_spinal_diagram.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The location of <a href="/wiki/Motor_neuron" title="Motor neuron">motor neurons</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anterior_horn_(spinal_cord)" title="Anterior horn (spinal cord)">anterior horn cells</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinal_column" title="Spinal column">spinal column</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Spinal polio, the most common form of paralytic poliomyelitis, results from viral invasion of the motor neurons of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anterior_horn_(spinal_cord)" title="Anterior horn (spinal cord)">anterior horn cells</a>, or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ventral" title="Ventral">ventral</a> (front) <a href="/wiki/Grey_matter" title="Grey matter">grey matter</a> section in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinal_column" title="Spinal column">spinal column</a>, which are responsible for movement of the muscles, including those of the <a href="/wiki/Torso" title="Torso">trunk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Limb_(anatomy)" title="Limb (anatomy)">limbs</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Intercostal_muscle" title="Intercostal muscle">intercostal muscles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henry1_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Henry1-15">[15]</a></sup> Virus invasion causes inflammation of the nerve cells, leading to damage or destruction of motor neuron <a href="/wiki/Ganglion" title="Ganglion">ganglia</a>. When spinal neurons die, <a href="/wiki/Wallerian_degeneration" title="Wallerian degeneration">Wallerian degeneration</a> takes place, leading to weakness of those muscles formerly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Innervate" title="Innervate">innervated</a> by the now-dead neurons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> With the destruction of nerve cells, the muscles no longer receive signals from the brain or spinal cord; without nerve stimulation, the muscles <a href="/wiki/Atrophy" title="Atrophy">atrophy</a>, becoming weak, floppy and poorly controlled, and finally completely paralyzed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henry1_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Henry1-15">[15]</a></sup> Maximum paralysis progresses rapidly (two to four days), and usually involves fever and muscle pain. Deep <a href="/wiki/Stretch_reflex" title="Stretch reflex">tendon</a> <a href="/wiki/Reflex" title="Reflex">reflexes</a> are also affected, and are typically absent or diminished; <a href="/wiki/Sense" title="Sense">sensation</a> (the ability to feel) in the paralyzed limbs, however, is not affected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>The extent of spinal paralysis depends on the region of the cord affected, which may be <a href="/wiki/Neck" title="Neck">cervical</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_thorax" title="Human thorax">thoracic</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Lumbar" title="Lumbar">lumbar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guide_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guide-43">[43]</a></sup> The virus may affect muscles on both sides of the body, but more often the paralysis is <a href="/wiki/Asymmetry" title="Asymmetry">asymmetrical</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baron_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Baron-33">[33]</a></sup> Any <a href="/wiki/Limb_(anatomy)" title="Limb (anatomy)">limb</a> or combination of limbs may be affected—one leg, one arm, or both legs and both arms. Paralysis is often more severe <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Proximally&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Proximally (page does not exist)">proximally</a> (where the limb joins the body) than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distally" title="Distally">distally</a> (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fingertip" title="Fingertip">fingertips</a> and <a href="/wiki/Toe" title="Toe">toes</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baron_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-Baron-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bulbar_polio">Bulbar polio</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brain_bulbar_region.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="299" data-file-width="295" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Brain_bulbar_region.svg/220px-Brain_bulbar_region.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Brain_bulbar_region.svg/330px-Brain_bulbar_region.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Brain_bulbar_region.svg/440px-Brain_bulbar_region.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brain_bulbar_region.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The location and anatomy of the bulbar region (in orange)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Making up about 2% of cases of paralytic polio, bulbar polio occurs when poliovirus invades and destroys nerves within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bulbar" title="Bulbar">bulbar</a> region of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brain_stem" title="Brain stem">brain stem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-14"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup> The bulbar region is a <a href="/wiki/White_matter" title="White matter">white matter</a> pathway that connects the <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_cortex" title="Cerebral cortex">cerebral cortex</a> to the brain stem. The destruction of these nerves weakens the muscles supplied by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cranial_nerve" title="Cranial nerve">cranial nerves</a>, producing symptoms of <a href="/wiki/Encephalitis" title="Encephalitis">encephalitis</a>, and causes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dyspnea" title="Dyspnea">difficulty breathing</a>, speaking and swallowing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Late_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Late-14">[14]</a></sup> Critical nerves affected are the <a href="/wiki/Glossopharyngeal_nerve" title="Glossopharyngeal nerve">glossopharyngeal nerve</a> (which partially controls swallowing and functions in the throat, tongue movement, and taste), the <a href="/wiki/Vagus_nerve" title="Vagus nerve">vagus nerve</a> (which sends signals to the heart, intestines, and lungs), and the <a href="/wiki/Accessory_nerve" title="Accessory nerve">accessory nerve</a> (which controls upper neck movement). Due to the effect on swallowing, secretions of <a href="/wiki/Mucus" title="Mucus">mucus</a> may build up in the airway, causing suffocation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Silverstein_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Silverstein-37">[37]</a></sup> Other signs and symptoms include <a href="/wiki/Facial_weakness" title="Facial weakness">facial weakness</a> (caused by destruction of the <a href="/wiki/Trigeminal_nerve" title="Trigeminal nerve">trigeminal nerve</a> and <a href="/wiki/Facial_nerve" title="Facial nerve">facial nerve</a>, which innervate the cheeks, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tear_duct" title="Tear duct">tear ducts</a>, gums, and muscles of the face, among other structures), <a href="/wiki/Diplopia" title="Diplopia">double vision</a>, difficulty in chewing, and abnormal <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_rate" title="Respiratory rate">respiratory rate</a>, depth, and rhythm (which may lead to <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_arrest" title="Respiratory arrest">respiratory arrest</a>). <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_edema" title="Pulmonary edema">Pulmonary edema</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shock_(circulatory)" title="Shock (circulatory)">shock</a> are also possible and may be fatal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guide_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Guide-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bulbospinal_polio">Bulbospinal polio</span></h4>
<p>Approximately 19% of all paralytic polio cases have both bulbar and spinal symptoms; this subtype is called respiratory or bulbospinal polio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-15"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup> Here, the virus affects the upper part of the cervical spinal cord (<a href="/wiki/Cervical_vertebrae" title="Cervical vertebrae">cervical vertebrae</a> C3 through C5), and paralysis of the <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_diaphragm" title="Thoracic diaphragm">diaphragm</a> occurs. The critical nerves affected are the <a href="/wiki/Phrenic_nerve" title="Phrenic nerve">phrenic nerve</a> (which drives the diaphragm to inflate the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_lung" title="Human lung">lungs</a>) and those that drive the muscles needed for swallowing. By destroying these nerves, this form of polio affects breathing, making it difficult or impossible for the patient to breathe without the support of a <a href="/wiki/Medical_ventilator" title="Medical ventilator">ventilator</a>. It can lead to paralysis of the arms and legs and may also affect swallowing and heart functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoyt_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoyt-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span></h2>
<p>Paralytic poliomyelitis may be clinically suspected in individuals experiencing acute onset of flaccid paralysis in one or more limbs with decreased or absent tendon reflexes in the affected limbs that cannot be attributed to another apparent cause, and without sensory or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cognitive" title="Cognitive">cognitive</a> loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>A laboratory diagnosis is usually made based on recovery of poliovirus from a stool sample or a swab of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_pharynx" title="Human pharynx">pharynx</a>. <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">Antibodies</a> to poliovirus can be diagnostic, and are generally detected in the blood of infected patients early in the course of infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-16"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup> Analysis of the patient's <a href="/wiki/Cerebrospinal_fluid" title="Cerebrospinal fluid">cerebrospinal fluid</a> (CSF), which is collected by a <a href="/wiki/Lumbar_puncture" title="Lumbar puncture">lumbar puncture</a> ("spinal tap"), reveals an increased number of <a href="/wiki/White_blood_cell" title="White blood cell">white blood cells</a> (primarily <a href="/wiki/Lymphocyte" title="Lymphocyte">lymphocytes</a>) and a mildly elevated protein level. Detection of virus in the CSF is diagnostic of paralytic polio, but rarely occurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-17"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>If poliovirus is isolated from a patient experiencing acute flaccid paralysis, it is further tested through <a href="/wiki/Oligonucleotide" title="Oligonucleotide">oligonucleotide</a> mapping (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetic_fingerprint" title="Genetic fingerprint">genetic fingerprinting</a>), or more recently by <a href="/wiki/Polymerase_chain_reaction" title="Polymerase chain reaction">PCR</a> amplification, to determine whether it is "<a href="/wiki/Wild_type" title="Wild type">wild type</a>" (that is, the virus encountered in nature) or "vaccine type" (derived from a strain of poliovirus used to produce polio vaccine).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> It is important to determine the source of the virus because for each reported case of paralytic polio caused by wild poliovirus, an estimated 200 to 3,000 other contagious <a href="/wiki/Asymptomatic_carrier" title="Asymptomatic carrier">asymptomatic carriers</a> exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Passive_immunization">Passive immunization</span></h3>
<p>In 1950, <a href="/wiki/William_Hammon" title="William Hammon">William Hammon</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pittsburgh" title="University of Pittsburgh">University of Pittsburgh</a> purified the <a href="/wiki/Gamma_globulin" title="Gamma globulin">gamma globulin</a> component of the <a href="/wiki/Blood_plasma" title="Blood plasma">blood plasma</a> of polio survivors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammon_1955_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hammon_1955-48">[48]</a></sup> Hammon proposed the gamma globulin, which contained antibodies to poliovirus, could be used to halt poliovirus infection, prevent disease, and reduce the severity of disease in other patients who had contracted polio. The results of a large <a href="/wiki/Clinical_trial" title="Clinical trial">clinical trial</a> were promising; the gamma globulin was shown to be about 80% effective in preventing the development of paralytic poliomyelitis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> It was also shown to reduce the severity of the disease in patients who developed polio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammon_1955_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hammon_1955-48">[48]</a></sup> Due to the limited supply of blood plasma gamma globulin was later deemed impractical for widespread use and the medical community focused on the development of a polio vaccine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rinaldo_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rinaldo-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vaccine">Vaccine</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Polio_vaccine" title="Polio vaccine">Polio vaccine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Poliodrops.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="288" data-file-width="264" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Poliodrops.jpg/220px-Poliodrops.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/20/Poliodrops.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Poliodrops.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A child receiving an oral polio vaccine</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Two types of vaccine are used throughout the world to combat polio. Both types induce immunity to polio, efficiently blocking person-to-person transmission of wild poliovirus, thereby protecting both individual vaccine recipients and the wider community (so-called <a href="/wiki/Herd_immunity" title="Herd immunity">herd immunity</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fine_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fine-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first candidate <a href="/wiki/Polio_vaccine" title="Polio vaccine">polio vaccine</a>, based on one serotype of a live but <a href="/wiki/Attenuated_vaccine" title="Attenuated vaccine">attenuated (weakened) virus</a>, was developed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virologist" title="Virologist">virologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Hilary_Koprowski" title="Hilary Koprowski">Hilary Koprowski</a>. Koprowski's prototype vaccine was given to an eight-year-old boy on 27 February 1950.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Koprowski continued to work on the vaccine throughout the 1950s, leading to large-scale trials in the then <a href="/wiki/Belgian_Congo" title="Belgian Congo">Belgian Congo</a> and the vaccination of seven million children in Poland against serotypes PV1 and PV3 between 1958 and 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sanofi_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sanofi-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>The second inactivated virus vaccine was developed in 1952 by <a href="/wiki/Jonas_Salk" title="Jonas Salk">Jonas Salk</a> at the University of Pittsburgh, and announced to the world on 12 April 1955.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spice_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spice-54">[54]</a></sup> The Salk vaccine, or inactivated poliovirus vaccine (IPV), is based on poliovirus grown in a type of monkey kidney <a href="/wiki/Tissue_culture" title="Tissue culture">tissue culture</a> (<a href="/wiki/Vero_cell" title="Vero cell">vero cell</a> <a href="/wiki/Cell_culture" title="Cell culture">line</a>), which is chemically inactivated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Formalin" title="Formalin">formalin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kew_2005_21-5"><a href="#cite_note-Kew_2005-21">[21]</a></sup> After two doses of IPV (given by <a href="/wiki/Injection_(medicine)" title="Injection (medicine)">injection</a>), 90% or more of individuals develop protective antibody to all three <a href="/wiki/Serotype" title="Serotype">serotypes</a> of poliovirus, and at least 99% are immune to poliovirus following three doses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-18"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Subsequently, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Sabin" title="Albert Sabin">Albert Sabin</a> developed another live, oral polio vaccine (OPV). It was produced by the repeated passage of the virus through nonhuman cells at sub<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physiological" title="Physiological">physiological</a> temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sabin_1973_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sabin_1973-55">[55]</a></sup> The attenuated poliovirus in the Sabin vaccine replicates very efficiently in the gut, the primary site of wild poliovirus infection and replication, but the vaccine strain is unable to replicate efficiently within <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous system</a> tissue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> A single dose of Sabin's oral polio vaccine produces immunity to all three poliovirus serotypes in about 50% of recipients. Three doses of live-attenuated OPV produce protective antibody to all three poliovirus types in more than 95% of recipients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-19"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Clinical_trial" title="Clinical trial">Human trials</a> of Sabin's vaccine began in 1957,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScienceOdyssey_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceOdyssey-57">[57]</a></sup> and in 1958 it was selected, in competition with the live vaccines of Koprowski and other researchers, by the US National Institutes of Health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sanofi_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sanofi-53">[53]</a></sup> Licensed in 1962,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScienceOdyssey_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceOdyssey-57">[57]</a></sup> it rapidly became the only polio vaccine used worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sanofi_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sanofi-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because OPV is inexpensive, easy to administer, and produces excellent immunity in the intestine (which helps prevent infection with wild virus in areas where it is <a href="/wiki/Endemic_(epidemiology)" title="Endemic (epidemiology)">endemic</a>), it has been the vaccine of choice for controlling poliomyelitis in many countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peds_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peds-58">[58]</a></sup> On very rare occasions (about one case per 750,000 vaccine recipients), the attenuated virus in OPV reverts into a form that can paralyze.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Racaniello_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-Racaniello-24">[24]</a></sup> Most <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">industrialized countries</a> have switched to IPV, which cannot revert, either as the sole vaccine against poliomyelitis or in combination with oral polio vaccine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment">Treatment</span></h2>
<p>There is no <a href="/wiki/Cure" title="Cure">cure</a> for polio. The focus of modern treatment has been on providing relief of symptoms, speeding recovery and preventing complications. Supportive measures include <a href="/wiki/Antibiotics" title="Antibiotics">antibiotics</a> to prevent infections in weakened muscles, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analgesics" title="Analgesics">analgesics</a> for pain, moderate exercise and a nutritious diet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daniel_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Daniel-60">[60]</a></sup> Treatment of polio often requires long-term rehabilitation, including <a href="/wiki/Occupational_therapy" title="Occupational therapy">occupational therapy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physical_therapy" title="Physical therapy">physical therapy</a>, braces, corrective shoes and, in some cases, <a href="/wiki/Orthopedic_surgery" title="Orthopedic surgery">orthopedic surgery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guide_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-Guide-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Portable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ventilator" title="Ventilator">ventilators</a> may be required to support breathing. Historically, a noninvasive, negative-pressure ventilator, more commonly called an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negative_pressure_ventilator" title="Negative pressure ventilator">iron lung</a>, was used to artificially maintain respiration during an acute polio infection until a person could breathe independently (generally about one to two weeks). Today, many polio survivors with permanent respiratory paralysis use modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biphasic_Cuirass_Ventilation" title="Biphasic Cuirass Ventilation">jacket-type</a> negative-pressure ventilators worn over the chest and abdomen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldberg_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldberg-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other <a href="/wiki/History_of_poliomyelitis#Historical_treatments" title="History of poliomyelitis">historical treatments for polio</a> include <a href="/wiki/Hydrotherapy" title="Hydrotherapy">hydrotherapy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electrotherapy" title="Electrotherapy">electrotherapy</a>, massage and passive motion exercises, and surgical treatments, such as tendon lengthening and nerve grafting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henry1_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Henry1-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prognosis">Prognosis</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polio_sequelle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1076" data-file-width="700" height="338" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Polio_sequelle.jpg/220px-Polio_sequelle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Polio_sequelle.jpg/330px-Polio_sequelle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Polio_sequelle.jpg/440px-Polio_sequelle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polio_sequelle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A child with a deformity of her right leg due to polio</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Patients with abortive polio infections recover completely. In those who develop only aseptic meningitis, the symptoms can be expected to persist for two to ten days, followed by complete recovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neumann_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Neumann-62">[62]</a></sup> In cases of spinal polio, if the affected nerve cells are completely destroyed, paralysis will be permanent; cells that are not destroyed, but lose function temporarily, may recover within four to six weeks after onset.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neumann_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Neumann-62">[62]</a></sup> Half the patients with spinal polio recover fully; one-quarter recover with mild disability, and the remaining quarter are left with severe disability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The degree of both acute paralysis and residual paralysis is likely to be proportional to the degree of <a href="/wiki/Viremia" title="Viremia">viremia</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inversely_proportional" title="Inversely proportional">inversely proportional</a> to the degree of <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">immunity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mueller_36-4"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-36">[36]</a></sup> Spinal polio is rarely fatal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Silverstein_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-Silverstein-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Without respiratory support, consequences of poliomyelitis with <a href="/wiki/Respiration_(physiology)" title="Respiration (physiology)">respiratory</a> involvement include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suffocation" title="Suffocation">suffocation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Aspiration_pneumonia" title="Aspiration pneumonia">pneumonia from aspiration of secretions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldberg_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goldberg-61">[61]</a></sup> Overall, 5–10% of patients with paralytic polio die due to the paralysis of muscles used for breathing. The <a href="/wiki/Case_fatality_rate" title="Case fatality rate">case fatality rate</a> (CFR) varies by age: 2–5% of children and up to 15–30% of adults die.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-20"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup> Bulbar polio often causes death if respiratory support is not provided;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoyt_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hoyt-44">[44]</a></sup> with support, its CFR ranges from 25 to 75%, depending on the age of the patient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-21"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> When intermittent positive pressure ventilation is available, the fatalities can be reduced to 15%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wackers_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wackers-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recovery">Recovery</span></h3>
<p>Many cases of poliomyelitis result in only temporary paralysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henry1_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-Henry1-15">[15]</a></sup> Nerve impulses return to the formerly paralyzed muscle within a month, and recovery is usually complete in six to eight months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neumann_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-Neumann-62">[62]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Neurophysiology" title="Neurophysiology">neurophysiological</a> processes involved in recovery following acute paralytic poliomyelitis are quite effective; muscles are able to retain normal strength even if half the original motor neurons have been lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Paralysis remaining after one year is likely to be permanent, although modest recoveries of muscle strength are possible 12 to 18 months after infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neumann_62-3"><a href="#cite_note-Neumann-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>One mechanism involved in recovery is nerve terminal sprouting, in which remaining brainstem and spinal cord motor neurons develop new branches, or axonal sprouts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> These sprouts can <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reinnervate" title="Reinnervate">reinnervate</a> orphaned muscle fibers that have been denervated by acute polio infection,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agre_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Agre-68">[68]</a></sup> restoring the fibers' capacity to contract and improving strength.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Terminal sprouting may generate a few significantly enlarged motor neurons doing work previously performed by as many as four or five units:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gawne_1995_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gawne_1995-38">[38]</a></sup> a single motor neuron that once controlled 200 muscle cells might control 800 to 1000 cells. Other mechanisms that occur during the rehabilitation phase, and contribute to muscle strength restoration, include <a href="/wiki/Muscle_hypertrophy" title="Muscle hypertrophy">myofiber hypertrophy</a>—enlargement of muscle fibers through exercise and activity—and transformation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle_fiber#Type_II" title="Muscle fiber">type II muscle fibers</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle_fiber#Type_I" title="Muscle fiber">type I muscle fibers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agre_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Agre-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grimby_1989_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grimby_1989-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to these physiological processes, the body possesses a number of compensatory mechanisms to maintain function in the presence of residual paralysis. These include the use of weaker muscles at a higher than usual intensity relative to the <a href="/wiki/Muscle_contraction#Contractions.2C_by_muscle_type" title="Muscle contraction">muscle's maximal capacity</a>, enhancing athletic development of previously little-used muscles, and using <a href="/wiki/Ligament" title="Ligament">ligaments</a> for stability, which enables greater mobility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grimby_1989_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grimby_1989-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Complications">Complications</span></h3>
<p>Residual complications of paralytic polio often occur following the initial recovery process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Late_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Late-14">[14]</a></sup> Muscle <a href="/wiki/Paresis" title="Paresis">paresis</a> and paralysis can sometimes result in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skeletal" title="Skeletal">skeletal</a> deformities, tightening of the joints and movement disability. Once the muscles in the limb become flaccid, they may interfere with the function of other muscles. A typical manifestation of this problem is <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Equinus_foot&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Equinus foot (page does not exist)">equinus foot</a> (similar to <a href="/wiki/Club_foot" title="Club foot">club foot</a>). This deformity develops when the muscles that pull the toes downward are working, but those that pull it upward are not, and the foot naturally tends to drop toward the ground. If the problem is left untreated, the <a href="/wiki/Achilles_tendon" title="Achilles tendon">Achilles tendons</a> at the back of the foot retract and the foot cannot take on a normal position. Polio victims that develop equinus foot cannot walk properly because they cannot put their heel on the ground. A similar situation can develop if the arms become paralyzed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aftereffects_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aftereffects-71">[71]</a></sup> In some cases the growth of an affected leg is slowed by polio, while the other leg continues to grow normally. The result is that one leg is shorter than the other and the person limps and leans to one side, in turn leading to deformities of the spine (such as <a href="/wiki/Scoliosis" title="Scoliosis">scoliosis</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aftereffects_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aftereffects-71">[71]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Osteoporosis" title="Osteoporosis">Osteoporosis</a> and increased likelihood of <a href="/wiki/Bone_fracture" title="Bone fracture">bone fractures</a> may occur. An intervention to prevent or lessen length disparity can be to perform an <a href="/wiki/Epiphysiodesis" title="Epiphysiodesis">epiphysiodesis</a> on the distal femoral and proximal tibial/fibular condyles, so that limb's growth is artificially stunted, and by the time of <a href="/wiki/Epiphyseal_plate" title="Epiphyseal plate">epiphyseal (growth) plate</a> closure, the legs are more equal in length. Alternatively, a person can be fitted with custom made footwear which corrects the difference in leg lengths. Other surgery to re-balance muscular agonist/antagonist imbalances may also be helpful. Extended use of braces or wheelchairs may cause compression <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neuropathy" title="Neuropathy">neuropathy</a>, as well as a loss of proper function of the <a href="/wiki/Vein" title="Vein">veins</a> in the legs, due to pooling of blood in paralyzed lower limbs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoyt_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hoyt-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MayoComps_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-MayoComps-72">[72]</a></sup> Complications from prolonged immobility involving the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lungs" title="Lungs">lungs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidneys</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a> include <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_edema" title="Pulmonary edema">pulmonary edema</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aspiration_pneumonia" title="Aspiration pneumonia">aspiration pneumonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urinary_tract_infection" title="Urinary tract infection">urinary tract infections</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kidney_stone" title="Kidney stone">kidney stones</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paralytic_ileus" title="Paralytic ileus">paralytic ileus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myocarditis" title="Myocarditis">myocarditis</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cor_pulmonale" title="Cor pulmonale">cor pulmonale</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoyt_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hoyt-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MayoComps_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-MayoComps-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-polio_syndrome">Post-polio syndrome</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Post-polio_syndrome" title="Post-polio syndrome">Post-polio syndrome</a></div>
<p>Between 25% and 50% of individuals who have recovered from paralytic polio in childhood can develop additional symptoms decades after recovering from the acute infection,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NINDS_Post-Polio_Syndrome_Fact_Sheet_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-NINDS_Post-Polio_Syndrome_Fact_Sheet-73">[73]</a></sup> notably new muscle weakness and extreme fatigue. This condition is known as <a href="/wiki/Post-polio_syndrome" title="Post-polio syndrome">post-polio syndrome</a> (PPS) or post-polio sequelae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cashman_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cashman-74">[74]</a></sup> The symptoms of PPS are thought to involve a failure of the oversized <a href="/wiki/Motor_unit" title="Motor unit">motor units</a> created during the recovery phase of the paralytic disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ramlow_1992_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ramlow_1992-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Annals_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Annals-76">[76]</a></sup> Contributing factors that increase the risk of PPS include aging with loss of neuron units, the presence of a permanent residual impairment after recovery from the acute illness, and both overuse and disuse of neurons. PPS is a slow, progressive disease, and there is no specific treatment for it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cashman_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cashman-74">[74]</a></sup> Post-polio syndrome is not an infectious process, and persons experiencing the syndrome do not shed poliovirus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PinkBook2009_1-22"><a href="#cite_note-PinkBook2009-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
