<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Poliomyelitis_eradication" title="Poliomyelitis eradication">Poliomyelitis eradication</a></div>
<p>Following the widespread use of poliovirus vaccine in the mid-1950s, the incidence of poliomyelitis declined dramatically in many industrialized countries. A global effort to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eradication_of_infectious_disease" title="Eradication of infectious disease">eradicate</a> polio began in 1988, led by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>, <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Rotary_Foundation" title="The Rotary Foundation">The Rotary Foundation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> These efforts have reduced the number of annual diagnosed cases by 99.9%; from an estimated 350,000 cases in 1988 to a low of 483 cases in 2001, after which it remained at a level of about 1,000 - 2000 cases per year for a number of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eradication_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-eradication-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morbidity_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-morbidity-79">[79]</a></sup> In 2015, cases decreased to 98.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2015casecount_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015casecount-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Polio is one of only two diseases currently the subject of a global <a href="/wiki/Eradication_of_infectious_diseases" title="Eradication of infectious diseases">eradication program</a>, the other being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guinea_worm_disease" title="Guinea worm disease">Guinea worm disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> So far, the only diseases completely eradicated by humankind are <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>, declared so, in 1980,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_smallpox_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_smallpox-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Rinderpest" title="Rinderpest">rinderpest</a>, likewise, in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> A number of eradication milestones have already been reached, and several regions of the world have been certified polio-free.</p>
<p>A concern is the presence of circulating vaccine-derived polioviruses (cVDPV). The OPV is not perfect: while the genetic characteristics are carefully balanced to maximize efficacy and minimize virulence, it is possible for the OPV to mutate. As a result, persons given the OPV can acquire acute or chronic infections; or can transmit (circulate) mutated OPV to other people. It is likely that cVDPV cases will exceed wild cases in the near future, making it desirable to discontinue use of the OPV as soon as safely possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-circulating_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-circulating-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergency_declaration">Emergency declaration</span></h3>
<p>In April 2012, the World Health Assembly declared the completion of polio eradication a programmatic emergency for global public health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Americas">Americas</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> were declared polio-free in 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMWR_1994_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-MMWR_1994-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_Pacific">Western Pacific</span></h3>
<p>In 2000 polio was declared to have been officially eliminated in 37 Western Pacific countries, including China and Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pacific_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pacific-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D.27Souza_2002_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-D.27Souza_2002-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite eradication ten years prior, an outbreak was confirmed in China in September 2011 involving a strain prevalent in neighboring Pakistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> was declared polio-free in 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_Europe_2002_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_Europe_2002-91">[91]</a></sup> On 1 September, 2015 WHO confirmed 2 cases of circulating vaccine-derived poliovirus type 1 in Ukraine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_Europe_2015_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_Europe_2015-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South-East_Asia">South-East Asia</span></h3>
<p>The last case of polio in the region was in India in January 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Since January 2011, there have been no reported cases of the wild polio infections in India, and in February 2012 the country was taken off the WHO list of polio endemic countries. It was reported that if there are no cases of wild polio in the country for two more years, it would be declared as a polio-free country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>On March 27, 2014 the WHO announced the eradication of poliomyelitis in the South-East Asia Region, which includes eleven countries: <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maldives" title="Maldives">Maldives</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timor-Leste" title="Timor-Leste">Timor-Leste</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eradication1_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-eradication1-96">[96]</a></sup> With the addition of this region, 80 per cent of the world population lives in polio-free regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eradication1_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-eradication1-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Syria">Syria</span></h3>
<p>In 2015, polio was believed to remain <a href="/wiki/Endemic_(epidemiology)" title="Endemic (epidemiology)">naturally spreading</a> in only two countries, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eradication1_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-eradication1-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GlobalPolio2015_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-GlobalPolio2015-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> although it continued to cause epidemics in other nearby countries due to hidden or reestablished transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> difficulties in executing immunization programs in the ongoing <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War" title="Syrian Civil War">civil war</a> led to a return of polio, probably in 2012,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> acknowledged by the WHO in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> 15 cases were confirmed among children in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> between October and November 2013 in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deir_Ezzor" title="Deir Ezzor">Deir Ezzor</a>. Later, two more cases, each one in rural <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">Aleppo</a>, were identified. It was the first outbreak in Syria since 1999. Doctors and international public health agencies report more than 90 cases of polio in Syria, with fears of contagion in rebel areas from lack of sanitation and safe-water services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suppressed_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Suppressed-104">[104]</a></sup> In May 2014, the World Health Organization declared polio's renewed spread a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PHEIC" title="PHEIC">world health emergency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newscientist.com_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-newscientist.com-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>A vaccination campaign in Syria operated literally under fire and led to the deaths of several vaccinators,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> but returned vaccination coverage to pre-war levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> No new cases have been reported since January 2014; vaccination continues, and Iraq is also being closely monitored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span></h3>
<p>In 2003 in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Region,_Nigeria" title="Northern Region, Nigeria">northern Nigeria</a>—a country which at that time was considered provisionally polio free—a <a href="/wiki/Fatwa" title="Fatwa">fatwa</a> was issued declaring that the polio vaccine was designed to render children sterile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Subsequently, polio reappeared in Nigeria and spread from there to several other countries. In 2013, nine health workers administering polio vaccine were targeted and killed by gunmen on motorcycles in <a href="/wiki/Kano" title="Kano">Kano</a>, but this was the first<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> and last attack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Local traditional and religious leaders and polio survivors worked to revive the campaign,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reuters.com_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-reuters.com-114">[114]</a></sup> and Nigeria has not had a polio case since July 24, 2014. In September 2015, Nigeria was removed from the polio-endemic list.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> In 2016 two cases recurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nigeria2016_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nigeria2016-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2013 the Center for Disease Control received reports of 183 cases of polio in <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>, 14 in <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> and 8 cases in the <a href="/wiki/Somali_Region" title="Somali Region">Somali Region</a> of Ethiopia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cdcp_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-cdcp-116">[116]</a></sup> but Africa had no confirmed cases of wild poliovirus (WPV) in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GlobalPolio2015_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-GlobalPolio2015-97">[97]</a></sup> A case of circulating vaccine-derived poliovirus (cVDPV) type 2 was detected in <a href="/wiki/Siguiri" title="Siguiri">Siguiri</a> in <a href="/wiki/Guinea" title="Guinea">Guinea</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Kankan" title="Kankan">Kankan</a> region, in August 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gpe2015_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-gpe2015-117">[117]</a></sup> In September 2015, a case of cVDPV in Mali was confirmed by the WHO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gpe2015_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-gpe2015-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Afghanistan_and_Pakistan">Afghanistan and Pakistan</span></h3>
<p>This is the last remaining region with wild polio cases. Both major sides of the Afghan civil war support polio vaccination<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> and polio rates are declining rapidly in Afghanistan, with only 19 cases in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GlobalPolio2015_97-2"><a href="#cite_note-GlobalPolio2015-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reuters.com_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-reuters.com-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> there were 53 cases in 2015, the highest number for any country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GlobalPolio2015_97-3"><a href="#cite_note-GlobalPolio2015-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reuters.com_114-2"><a href="#cite_note-reuters.com-114">[114]</a></sup> Vaccination in Pakistan is hindered by conflict and organizational problems. The militant <a href="/wiki/Tehrik-i-Taliban_Pakistan" title="Tehrik-i-Taliban Pakistan">Pakistani Taliban</a> claims vaccination is a Western plot to <a href="/wiki/Sterilization_(medicine)" title="Sterilization (medicine)">sterilise</a> local children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC2016_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2016-119">[119]</a></sup> 66 vaccinators were killed in 2013 and 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thenational.ae_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-thenational.ae-121">[121]</a></sup> Cases have dropped by 70% in 2015; reasons include Dh440 million support from the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a> to vaccinate more than ten million children,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thenational.ae_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-thenational.ae-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> changes in the military situation, and arrests of some of those who attacked polio workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC2016_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2016-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_poliomyelitis" title="History of poliomyelitis">History of poliomyelitis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polio_Egyptian_Stele.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="703" data-file-width="500" height="309" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Polio_Egyptian_Stele.jpg/220px-Polio_Egyptian_Stele.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Polio_Egyptian_Stele.jpg/330px-Polio_Egyptian_Stele.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Polio_Egyptian_Stele.jpg/440px-Polio_Egyptian_Stele.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polio_Egyptian_Stele.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egyptian</a> <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stele</a> thought to represent a polio victim, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/18th_Dynasty" title="18th Dynasty">18th Dynasty</a> (1403–1365 BC)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The effects of polio have been known since <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistory</a>; <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptian</a> paintings and carvings depict otherwise healthy people with withered limbs, and children walking with canes at a young age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paul_1971_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paul_1971-124">[124]</a></sup> The first clinical description was provided by the English physician <a href="/wiki/Michael_Underwood_(physician)" title="Michael Underwood (physician)">Michael Underwood</a> in 1789, where he refers to polio as "a debility of the lower extremities".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Underwood_1789_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Underwood_1789-125">[125]</a></sup> The work of physicians <a href="/wiki/Jakob_Heine" title="Jakob Heine">Jakob Heine</a> in 1840 and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Oskar_Medin" title="Karl Oskar Medin">Karl Oskar Medin</a> in 1890 led to it being known as <i>Heine–Medin disease</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pearce_2005_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pearce_2005-126">[126]</a></sup> The disease was later called <i>infantile paralysis</i>, based on its propensity to affect children.</p>
<p>Before the 20th century, polio infections were rarely seen in infants before six months of age, most cases occurring in children six months to four years of age. Poorer <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a> of the time resulted in a constant exposure to the virus, which enhanced a natural <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">immunity</a> within the population. In developed countries during the late 19th and early 20th centuries, improvements were made in community sanitation, including better <a href="/wiki/Sewage" title="Sewage">sewage</a> disposal and clean water supplies. These changes drastically increased the proportion of children and adults at risk of paralytic polio infection, by reducing childhood exposure and immunity to the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robertson_1993_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robertson_1993-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>Small localized paralytic polio <a href="/wiki/Epidemic" title="Epidemic">epidemics</a> began to appear in Europe and the United States around 1900.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trevelyan_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trevelyan-128">[128]</a></sup> Outbreaks reached <a href="/wiki/Pandemic" title="Pandemic">pandemic</a> proportions in Europe, North America, Australia, and New Zealand during the first half of the 20th century. By 1950 the peak age incidence of paralytic poliomyelitis in the United States had shifted from infants to children aged five to nine years, when the risk of paralysis is greater; about one-third of the cases were reported in persons over 15 years of age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melnick_1990_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Melnick_1990-129">[129]</a></sup> Accordingly, the rate of paralysis and death due to polio infection also increased during this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trevelyan_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Trevelyan-128">[128]</a></sup> In the United States, the 1952 polio epidemic became the worst outbreak in the nation's history. Of nearly 58,000 cases reported that year 3,145 died and 21,269 were left with mild to disabling paralysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zamula_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zamula-130">[130]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Intensive_care_medicine" title="Intensive care medicine">Intensive care medicine</a> has its origin in the fight against polio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Most hospitals in the 1950s had limited access to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negative_pressure_ventilator" title="Negative pressure ventilator">iron lungs</a> for patients unable to breathe without mechanical assistance. Respiratory centers designed to assist the most severe polio patients, first established in 1952 at the Blegdam Hospital of <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen" title="Copenhagen">Copenhagen</a> by <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Danish</a> <a href="/wiki/Anesthesia" title="Anesthesia">anesthesiologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Bj%C3%B8rn_Aage_Ibsen" title="Bjørn Aage Ibsen">Bjørn Ibsen</a>, were the harbingers of subsequent <a href="/wiki/Intensive_care_unit" title="Intensive care unit">intensive care units</a> (ICU). (A year later, Ibsen would establish the world's first dedicated ICU.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>The polio epidemics not only altered the lives of those who survived them, but also brought profound cultural changes, spurring <a href="/wiki/Grassroots" title="Grassroots">grassroots</a> fund-raising campaigns that would revolutionize medical <a href="/wiki/Philanthropy" title="Philanthropy">philanthropy</a>, and giving rise to the modern field of <a href="/wiki/Physical_therapy" title="Physical therapy">rehabilitation therapy</a>. As one of the largest disabled groups in the world, polio survivors also helped to advance the modern <a href="/wiki/Disability_rights_movement" title="Disability rights movement">disability rights movement</a> through campaigns for the social and civil rights of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disabled" title="Disabled">disabled</a>. The World Health Organization estimates that there are 10 to 20 million polio survivors worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewsDesk_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-NewsDesk-133">[133]</a></sup> In 1977 there were 254,000 persons living in the United States who had been paralyzed by polio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> According to doctors and local polio support groups, some 40,000 polio survivors with varying degrees of paralysis live in Germany, 30,000 in Japan, 24,000 in France, 16,000 in Australia, 12,000 in Canada and 12,000 in the United Kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewsDesk_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-NewsDesk-133">[133]</a></sup> Many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_polio_survivors" title="List of polio survivors">notable individuals have survived polio</a> and often credit the prolonged immobility and residual paralysis associated with polio as a driving force in their lives and careers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>The disease was very well publicized during the polio epidemics of the 1950s, with extensive media coverage of any scientific advancements that might lead to a cure. Thus, the scientists working on polio became some of the most famous of the century. Fifteen scientists and two laymen who made important contributions to the knowledge and treatment of poliomyelitis are honored by the <a href="/wiki/Polio_Hall_of_Fame" title="Polio Hall of Fame">Polio Hall of Fame</a>, which was dedicated in 1957 at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roosevelt_Warm_Springs_Institute_for_Rehabilitation" title="Roosevelt Warm Springs Institute for Rehabilitation">Roosevelt Warm Springs Institute for Rehabilitation</a> in <a href="/wiki/Warm_Springs,_Georgia" title="Warm Springs, Georgia">Warm Springs, Georgia</a>, US. In 2008 four organizations (Rotary International, the World Health Organization, the U.S. Centers for Disease Control and UNICEF) were added to the Hall of Fame.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>World Polio Day (24 October) was established by <a href="/wiki/Rotary_International" title="Rotary International">Rotary International</a> to commemorate the birth of <a href="/wiki/Jonas_Salk" title="Jonas Salk">Jonas Salk</a>, who led the first team to develop a vaccine against poliomyelitis. Use of this inactivated poliovirus vaccine and subsequent widespread use of the oral poliovirus vaccine developed by <a href="/wiki/Albert_Sabin" title="Albert Sabin">Albert Sabin</a> led to establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Global_Polio_Eradication_Initiative" title="Global Polio Eradication Initiative">Global Polio Eradication Initiative</a> (GPEI) in 1988. Since then, GPEI has reduced polio worldwide by 99%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h3>
<p>The term derives from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Ancient Greek transliteration"><i>poliós</i></span> (<span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">πολιός</span>), meaning "grey", <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Ancient Greek transliteration"><i>myelós</i></span> (<span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">µυελός</span> “marrow”), referring to the grey matter of the <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">spinal cord</a>, and the suffix <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/-itis" title="wikt:-itis">-itis</a></i>, which denotes <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a>.,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chamberlin_2005_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-Chamberlin_2005-13">[13]</a></sup> i.e., inflammation of the spinal cord’s grey matter, although a severe infection can extend into the brainstem and even higher structures, resulting in <a href="/wiki/Polioencephalitis" title="Polioencephalitis">polio<i>encephal</i>itis</a>, producing a <a href="/wiki/Apnea" title="Apnea">lack of ability to breathe</a> that requires mechanical assistance such as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negative_pressure_ventilator" title="Negative pressure ventilator">iron lung</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span></h2>
<p>The Poliovirus Antivirals Initiative was launched in 2007 with the aim of developing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiviral_medication" title="Antiviral medication">antiviral medications</a> for polio, but while several promising candidates were identified, none have progressed beyond Phase II clinical trials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>

</ul>

<li id="cite_note-Suppressed-104"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Suppressed_104-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Sparrow, Annie (20 February 2014). <a class="external text" href="http://www.nybooks.com/articles/archives/2014/feb/20/syrias-polio-epidemic-suppressed-truth/?insrc=hpma&amp;utm_source=Sailthru&amp;utm_medium=email&amp;utm_term=*Mideast%20Brief&amp;utm_campaign=Mideast%20Brief%201-22-2014" rel="nofollow">"Syria's Polio Epidemic: The Suppressed Truth"</a>. <i>New York Review</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">23 January</span> 2014</span>. <q>Ninety or so afflicted children may sound like a small number, but they are only a tiny manifestation of an enormous problem, since for each crippled child up to one thousand more are silently infected. Polio is so contagious that a single case is considered a public health emergency. Ninety cases could mean some 90,000 people infected, each a carrier invisibly spreading the disease to others for weeks on end.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APoliomyelitis&amp;rft.atitle=Syria%99s+Polio+Epidemic%3A+The+Suppressed+Truth&amp;rft.aufirst=Annie&amp;rft.aulast=Sparrow&amp;rft.date=2014-02-20&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.nybooks.com%2Farticles%2Farchives%2F2014%2Ffeb%2F20%2Fsyrias-polio-epidemic-suppressed-truth%2F%3Finsrc%3Dhpma%26utm_source%3DSailthru%26utm_medium%3Demail%26utm_term%3D%2AMideast%2520Brief%26utm_campaign%3DMideast%2520Brief%25201-22-2014&amp;rft.jtitle=New+York+Review&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
